<div id="review_430893565" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/430893565" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9084332-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335481142p2/9084332.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/430893565?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/9084332-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer430893565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9888023333661889055">Solzhenitsyn's The First Circle<br/><br/>The novel's title refers to an island of respite in the archipelago of Stalinist Russia's gulag. It's a sharashka, a special kind of prison where you work at a desk and there is bread on the table; it's a camp for intellectuals, for minds that have been classified as useful to the state. There's even butter here, "for professors ... one and a half ounces and for engineers three-quarters of an ounce." The sharashka's not only a respite from the inhumanity of the cam</span>
  <span id="freeText9888023333661889055" style="display:none">Solzhenitsyn's The First Circle<br /><br />The novel's title refers to an island of respite in the archipelago of Stalinist Russia's gulag. It's a sharashka, a special kind of prison where you work at a desk and there is bread on the table; it's a camp for intellectuals, for minds that have been classified as useful to the state. There's even butter here, "for professors ... one and a half ounces and for engineers three-quarters of an ounce." The sharashka's not only a respite from the inhumanity of the camp system; it's also something of respite from tyranny itself. The prisoners--despite their enforced asceticism (sometimes itself a blessing) and the always imminent likelihood of their return to brutal camps they've only temporarily escaped--are at times freer than the guards and administrators that keep them, freer too than the thousand civil servants who wait by midnight telephones, tapping pens on blank pads of paper, staring out dark windows with the mute eyes of lidless aquarium-fishes--sleepless because Stalin is sleepless and, when he is awake, it's not that his janissaries may not, but (like men in a fairy-tale spell) they physically cannot sleep.<br /><br />Nevertheless, the sharashka, island of respite though it is, is still (according to its inmates) hell--it is "a concept [says Rubin, an honest-to-God-and-man, evangelizing Communist who loves his Hemingway and Goethe] ... thought up by Dante. ... It was a Christian's duty to toss those pagans into hell. But the Renaissance conscience couldn't reconcile itself to the idea of enlightened men being packed in with all sorts of sinners and condemned to physical torture. So Dante thought up a special place for them in hell. If you'll allow me . . . It's the fourth Canto and goes about like this<br /><br />At last we reached the base of a great Citadel ...<br /><br />Look around at the old arches here!<br /><br />... Circled by seven towering battlements<br />And by a sweet brook flowing all around them all ...<br /><br />You came here in the Black Maria, so you didn't see the gates--<br /><br />... I saw four mighty presences come toward us<br />With neither joy nor sorrow in their bearing.<br />... 'What souls are those whose merit lights their way<br />Even in Hell? What joy sets them apart?' "<br /><br />At work even in the loveless, inefficient machine of Stalin's state is a grace (actual but not saving) that nations at war extend to men of talent, setting them apart. And while merit does thus in some sense receive the honor due to it, it is also objectively insulted to subserve the opaque (but ultimately destructive) ends of invisible and invincible masters, and is inwardly embarrassed to embrace a reprieve denied to others. When all is said and done, then, these men "whose merits light their way, even in hell" are sufferers-with, even sufferers-for the rest of their peoples in a unique and uniquely human manner. In fact, the "joy that sets them apart" intensifies their experience of their peoples' predicament--even while that joy, in itself, is genuine and free, a pure and shining signal from beyond the reign of futility to which they and their peoples are subjected.<br /><br />The novel's fourth chapter shows its quiet, strong virtues:<br /><br />4<br /><br />Boogie-Woogie<br /><br />The Acoustics Laboratory was a wide, high-ceilinged room with several windows. It was disorderly and crowded with electronic instruments on plank shelves, shiny aluminum counters, assembly benches, new plywood cabinets from a Moscow factory, snug writing desks that had been war booty.<br /><br />Large overhead bulbs in frosted globes cast a pleasant dispersed white light.<br /><br />In a far corner of the room stood a sound-insulated acoustical booth. It looked only partially completed. On the outside, plain sacking had been tacked over straw. Its two-foot-thick solid door was open at the moment, and the woolen curtain over it had been thrown back to air the booth. Next to the booth, rows of brass plugs gleamed on the black bakelite face of the main switchboard.<br /><br />At a desk by the booth, her back to it, her narrow shoulders barely covered by a shawl, sat a tiny, frail girl with a stern face.<br /><br />All the other people in the room, ten or so, were all dressed in the same dark-blue coveralls. Lit by the overhead lights and by additional spots of light from flexible desk lamps, they tinkered, walked about, hammered, soldered, and sat at the assembly benches and writing desks.<br /><br />From different places in the room three radio receivers, cabinetless and put together on whatever aluminum chassis had come to hand, broadcast the conflicting rhythms of jazz, a piano concert, and folk songs of the East.<br /><br />Rubin walked slowly through the laboratory to his desk, still holding his Mongolian-Finnish dictionary and his Hemingway. There were white pastry crumbs on his curly black beard.<br /><br />Though the coveralls issued to prisoners were all identically made, they were worn in different ways. On Rubin's, one button had been ripped off, the belt was loosened, and folds of cloth hung at his stomach. On the other hand, a young fellow with flowing chestnut hair, who was at the moment blocking Rubin's way, wore the very same dark-blue coveralls like a dandy. His cloth belt was clinched tight around his thin waist, and he wore a blue silk shirt which, though faded from frequent washing, was closed with a bright necktie. This young man entirely blocked the side passage Rubin was trying to get through. In his right hand he brandished a hot soldering iron, and he had placed his left foot on a chair. Leaning on his knee, he concentrated on a radio diagram in a copy of the magazine Wireless Engineer, and sang at the same time:<br /><br />"Boogie-woogie, boogie-woogie.<br />Samba! Samba!<br />Boogie-woogie, boogie-woogie.<br />Samba! Samba!"<br /><br />Rubin could not get past him, and he stood there for a moment with a mock expression of meekness. The young man did not appear to notice him.<br /><br />"Valentulya," Rubin said, "couldn't you move your hind foot a bit?"<br /><br />Valentine, not looking up, answered in a strong clipped voice, "Lev Grigorich! You're interrupting. You're wasting time. Why come here at nights? What is there for you to do here?" He now looked at Rubin with bright young eyes full of surprise. "What the hell do we need philologists for! Ha, ha, ha!" he said ironically. "After all, you're no engineer! For shame!"<br /><br />Pursing his fleshy lips in a childlike pout and opening his eyes unbelievably wide, Rubin said, "My boy! There are all kinds of engineers. Some of them have built successful careers selling soda water."<br /><br />"Not me! I'm a first-class engineer! Take that into consideration, little man!" Valentine retorted sharply, placing the soldering iron in a wire stand and standing erect.<br /><br />He had the clean look of youth. Life had not stained his face. His movements were boyish. It was hard to believe that he had graduated from an institute before the war, survived German POW camps, been in Europe, and was now serving his fifth year of imprisonment in his own country.<br /><br />Rubin sighed. "Without duly attested references from Belgium the administration cannot--"<br /><br />"What references are you talking about?" Pyranchikov's brows flew up. "Ha, ha, ha! Just forget it! Consider--I love women madly!"<br /><br />The stern young woman near them could not control her smile.<br /><br />Another inmate, at a window near the passageway Rubin was trying to get through, put down his work and listened to Valentine with approval.<br /><br />"Only theoretically, it would appear," answered Rubin with a bored, meditative expression.<br /><br />"And I love spending money!"<br /><br />"But you don't have any."<br /><br />"Well, then how can I be a bad engineer? Just think: in order to love women--and different ones--all the time, I need a lot of money! And in order to have a lot of money I have to earn it! And to do that as an engineer I have to be brilliant in my field. And how can I do that if I'm not genuinely fascinated by it? Ha, ha! You look pale!"<br /><br />Wholehearted conviction shone in Valentine's face, raised challengingly to Rubin.<br /><br />"Ah-ha!" cried the zek next to the window, whose writing desk faced the young woman's. "Lev, come and hear how well I've caught Valentulya's voice! It has a bell-like quality! That's what I'm going to write in my report. Bell-like. You can recognize a voice like that on any phone. No matter how much interference there is."<br /><br />And he opened a big sheet ruled off in squares, on which there were columns of names followed by tree-shaped voice classifications.<br /><br />"What kind of nonsense is that?" Valentine said, brushing the remark aside. He took up his soldering iron, and rosin began to smoke.<br /><br />The passage opened up, and Rubin, on his way to his chair, paused to bend over the sheet of voice classifications.<br /><br />He and his friend Gleb Nerzhin looked at it together in silence.<br /><br />"We've made some real progress, Gleb," he said. "Used together with voice prints, it will make a good tool. Soon will be able to understand what a telephone voice depends on." He gave a start. "What's that on the radio?"<br /><br />The jazz was louder in the room, but a lilting, bubbling piano piece came through his own homemade receiver on the window sill, with a single melodic line which kept gleaming and disappearing.<br /><br />Nerzhin replied, "It's a miracle. That's Beethoven's Seventeenth Sonata in D Minor. For some reason it's never--listen, listen."<br /><br />They both bent close to the receiver, but the jazz interfered badly.<br /><br />"Valentine," said Gleb. "Please, let us listen! Show a little consideration!"<br /><br />"I've already shown a little consideration," Valentine said. "I knocked your receiver together. Now I'm going to unsolder your coil and you'll never find it."<br /><br />The young woman raised her severe brows and said, "Valentine Martynich! Really, it's impossible to listen to three radios at once. Turn yours off as you've been asked."<br /><br />Valentine's radio at this point was playing a slow fox trot, and the young woman secretly liked it very much.<br /><br />"Serafima Vitalyevna! That's monstrous!" He seized the back of a chair and gesticulated as if he were speaking from a platform. "How can a normal, healthy person of your age not enjoy energetic, invigorating jazz? You're all being corrupted by all kinds of old trash! Have you really never danced the 'Blue Tango?' Have you never seen the skits of Arkady Raikin? You just don't know the best that man has created! Worse than that--you've never been to Europe. Where could you possibly have learned to live? I advise you very, very seriously : you must fall in love with someone." He delivered this oratory from behind the chair, not noticing the bitter set of the young woman's lips. "Anyone--Ça depend de vous! Lights twinkling in the night. The rustle of stylish clothes."<br /><br />"He's gone out of phase again!" Rubin said with concern. "So we have to use force."<br /><br />And behind Valentine's back he turned off the jazz himself.<br /><br />Valentine turned around, stung. "Lev Grigorich, who gave you the right to do that?"<br /><br />He frowned and tried to look menacing. The free-flowing strains of the Seventeenth Sonata rose in all their purity, competing now only with the crude song from the third radio around the corner.<br /><br />Rubin's face slackened, showing yielding black eyes and a beard dotted with cake crumbs.<br /><br />"Engineer Pryanchikov! Are you still worrying about the Atlantic Charter? Have you written your will? Whom will you refuse to leave your bedroom slippers to?"<br /><br />Pryanchikov's face suddenly grew serious. He looked brightly into Rubin's eyes and said quietly,<br /><br />"Listen, what the hell! You're driving me crazy. A person at least ought to have some freedom in prison."<br /><br />He was called away by one of the assembly workers, and he left in a state of gloom.<br /><br />Rubin settled silently into his armchair, back to back with Gleb, and prepared to listen to the music. But the soothing, plunging melody broke off unexpectedly, like speech cut off in the middle of a word. And that was the unceremonious end of the Seventeenth Sonata.<br /><br />Rubin delivered himself of a string of oaths, comprehensible to Gleb alone.<br /><br />"Spell it out. I can't hear you," Gleb said, his back still turned.<br /><br />"That's my luck, I tell you," said Rubin hoarsely, not turning around either. "There you are--I missed the sonata and I've never heard it."<br /><br />"Because you're disorganized. How many times have I pounded it into you?" his friend declared. Just a minute before, when he was recording Pryanchikov's voice, he had been full of enthusiasm, and now he was listless and sad again. "And the sonata was very, very good. Why doesn't it have a name like the others? 'The Shining Sonata,' wouldn't that be right? Everything in it flashes, shines--good and bad, sad and merry, the way it does in life. And there's no ending . . . just like life. That's what it should be called the Ut in Vita Sonata. And where have you been?"<br /><br />"With the Germans. We were seeing Christmas in," Rubin said, grinning ironically.<br /><br />They talked without seeing each other, the backs of their heads almost touching.<br /><br />"Good man," Gleb thought, then aloud: "I like your attitude toward them. You spend hours teaching Max Russian. Yet you have every reason to hate them."<br /><br />"Hate? No, but my former love for them, of course, has been a bit tarnished. Even nonpolitical Max--doesn't he too, share some responsibility with the executioners? After all, he didn't do anything to stop them."<br /><br />"Just as we, right now, are not doing anything to stop Abakumov or Shishkin-Myshkin."<br /><br />"Listen, Gleb, once and for all, I am no more a Jew than I am a Russian. And I am no more a Russian than I am a citizen of the world!"<br /><br />"Well said! Citizens of the world! It sounds pure and bloodless."<br /><br />"In other words, cosmopolites. They were right to put us in prison."<br /><br />"Of course they were right. Even though you are always trying to prove the contrary to the Supreme Soviet."<br /><br />The radio on the window sill promised the "Daily List of Production Competitions" in thirty seconds.<br /><br />In the course of those thirty seconds, Gleb Nerzhin reached for the radio knob with calm determination and cut off the announcer's hoarse croak. His tired face was grayish.<br /><br />Valentine Pryanchikov was at this moment absorbed in a new problem. Calculating what series of amplifications to use, he sang to himself in a loud, carefree voice:<br /><br />"Boogie-woogie, boogie-woogie.<br />Samba! Samba!"<br /><br />[youtube=<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?feature=player_detailpage&amp;v=LfjD-DQ5REk">http://www.youtube.com/watch?feature=...</a>]</span>

<div id="review_2520418876" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2520418876" />
    <a title="Johnny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/903066-johnny"><img alt="Johnny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442457109p2/903066.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2520418876?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johnny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johnny" href="/user/show/903066-johnny">Johnny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903066-johnny?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2520418876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2202368614528152209">Fortunately, we now have the uncensored edition of Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn’s <i>In the First Circle</i>, first published in 1968. While I was more familiar with <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i>, I found <i>In the First Circle</i> to be easier to read—though similar themes appear. That makes sense when both novels are about the prison system where political prisoners such as Solzhenitsyn and others lived or died. I remember a passage in <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i> where the reader is told that the prisoner has already lost ever</span>
  <span id="freeText2202368614528152209" style="display:none">Fortunately, we now have the uncensored edition of Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn’s <i>In the First Circle</i>, first published in 1968. While I was more familiar with <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i>, I found <i>In the First Circle</i> to be easier to read—though similar themes appear. That makes sense when both novels are about the prison system where political prisoners such as Solzhenitsyn and others lived or died. I remember a passage in <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i> where the reader is told that the prisoner has already lost everything, and that if the prisoner recognizes that, even death would have no fear and his fearlessness would frighten his torturer. <i>In the First Circle</i> has a scene where a prisoner confronts a high-ranking official in the prison system, saying: “In fact, you should get it into your head, and pass it on to whoever needs to know up above, that your power depends on not taking absolutely everything from people. The man from whom you’ve taken everything is no longer in your power; he is free again.” (p. 95) <br /><br />The title, <i>In the First Circle</i>, comes from a casual conversation where a diplomat tries to explain the relation of the Soviet state to the rest of the world. After drawing a circle on the ground, using a stick, he said, “’You see this circle? That’s our country. That’s the first circle. Now here’s the second.’” Then, he draws a larger circle around the first. “’That is mankind at large. You would think that the first forms part of the second, wouldn’t you? Not in the least! There are barriers of prejudice. Not to mention barbed wire and machine guns. To break through, physically or spiritually, is well-nigh impossible. Which means that mankind, as such, does not exist. There are only fatherlands, everyone’s fatherland alien to everyone else’s….“ (p. 314). <br /><br />We also see a reference from which the title could derive in an assessment from one of the main characters as several prisoners are being transported away from the special prison to a work camp. “No, Ilya Terentich, that isn’t hell. <i>That</i> is no hell! Hell is where we’re going. We’re going back to hell. The special prison is the highest, the best, the first circle of hell. It’s practically paradise.” (p. 740) It also echoes a feeling that a condemned party leader, a prison official, reflects, “Stalin was always pleasant and affable when he talked to peripheral people. But you couldn’t break out of the inner circle; there was no way back.” (p. 131) At least, there was no way back if one intended to keep on living. In a style that reminds me of the whiplash one sometimes gets reading <i>War and Peace</i>, Solzhenitsyn does jump from character to character. In this case, however, there seems a deliberate attempt to juxtapose the powerful versus the prisoners. It seems like Solzhenitsyn accomplishes more within the change of character point-of-view, however.<br /><br />The plot for the book begins with an anonymous phone call from a Soviet diplomat to the U.S. Embassy. He attempts to communicate to the U.S. diplomat that the Soviets are going to get their hands on the atomic bomb. Meanwhile, a prolific excursus takes one through the prison system to <i>sharaskas</i> (special facilities where imprisoned scientists and technicians are working on a project to create a phone that will automatically encode the transmissions to protect state secrets and sensitive information from being overheard). This project ends up dovetailing with a project to read voiceprints and identify vocal characteristics from a print-out. “Phonoscopy” is the word coined by the inventor to parallel “dactyloscopy” (fingerprinting) as a new forensic science (p. 247). <br /><br />It often seems that the prisoners have the better deal than these paranoid prison authorities. I particularly liked a prison yard conversation between two scientists. Initially, one is speaking of the value of difficulty in research, but eventually, one senses that he is also speaking of life: “In the sphere of the unknown, we should regard difficulties as a hidden treasure! As a rule, the more difficult things are, the more they can help us.” (p. 182) But the prison camp philosopher goes on and, a page later, observes: “If great exertions have already been made, setbacks are a still greater cause for rejoicing! They mean that our iron crowbar has struck the iron treasure chest! And overcoming increased difficulties is all the more valuable in that the agent grows in stature with each setback, grows in proportion to the difficulties he meets.” (p. 183)<br /><br />There is a moving section in the middle of the book where these victims of political trials hold a mock trial (for entertainment) of prince from 750 years ago. Yet, the entertainment quickly becomes a parody of the judicial farces that the inmates have been forced to go through. For many it was cathartic, and for others, it was a sad reminder of their past and their dim, unlikely future. For many of the inmates, it seems like the system denies their existence. One wife “…was notified not that he was dead but that he was missing. Ever eager to arrest people, the state always shied away from admitting it.” (p. 257) <br /><br />But don’t think that Solzhenitsyn’s expose of Soviet failings left out the failings of democracy, as well. Though the inmate is philosophizing about the Communist Party, it seems like all political parties are tainted by the following brush stroke, “Party membership always means leveling down to the majority, accepting discipline, saying things you don’t mean. Every party warps the individual and perverts justice.” (p. 666) Earlier in the novel, one character’s uncle is described (as my late father somewhat described himself regarding our own government), “He had never joined a political party, because all party programs, as he saw it, did violence to man’s free will, and he did not acknowledge the prophetic superiority of party leaders to the rest of mankind.” (p. 452) <br /><br />There is an interesting phrasing tantamount to what we call “Murphy’s Law.” It read, “The more material involved in a historical event, the greater the likelihood of individual incidental errors—judicial, tactical, ideological, economic, or whatever.” (p. 321). At another point, an imprisoned artist protests, “Who can know for sure whether life molds man or the strong and noble human being molds life?” (p. 333) But perhaps the most profound idea in the book was the folk ethic described by one prisoner, “I can tell you: killing wolves is right; eating people is wrong.” He explains what he means almost immediately, “The wolf killer is in the right; the man-eater is wrong.” (both on p. 511) Another observation also rings true. One inmate warns the other of the dangers in the expression that the ends justify the means: “Just remember this, then—that the loftier you aim, the nobler must be your means. Treacherous means destroy the end itself.” (p. 515)<br /><br />As one can imagine, there are pointed philosophical and political discussions and observations throughout the novel. There are also some sentences that read extremely well, even in translation. I liked, “A generation brought up to be suspicious and secretive could imagine mysteries when there were none.” (p. 258) That seems to characterize the Cold War on both sides. I also liked this idealistic comment about Russian women: “They are consorts of Vikings, the bright-faced Isoldes with adamantine souls.” (p. 231) Another image haunted me when I read, “…his face was that of an actor jaded by too many performances, and his vague blue-gray eyes seemed clouded by sadness.” (p. 607) Indeed, in a self-absorbed sense, I identified with, “He had grown used to being a sheepdog in a pack of wolves, defending himself single-handedly against forty or fifty at a time.” (p. 514)<br /><br />And, for those so inclined, one can find plenty of references to Christianity in the novel. A prisoner states: “We ridicule the Christians—silly so-and-sos, living in hopes of paradise and putting up with absolutely everything on this earth. But what have we got to look forward to. For whose sake do we suffer? For our mythical descendants? What difference does it make whether it’s happiness for posterity or happiness in the next world? We can’t see either.” (p. 341) Earlier, in a debate between two prisoners, one states: “They who want to destroy Christianity, and only they, would have it become the creed of eunuchs. But Christianity is the faith of the strong in spirit. We must have the courage to see the evil in the world and to root it out. Wait a while—you, too, will come to God. Your refusal to believe in anything is no position for a thinking man; it’s just spiritual poverty.” (p. 178) <br /><br />Here’s another jewel. As the novel is moving toward resolution, and one’s suspense as to whether the one who made the telephone call to the U.S. Embassy was going to be arrested is settled, the “traitor” observes: “In history one side always wins—but never only one side’s ideas! Ideas merge; they have a life of their own. The victor always borrows something, sometimes a lot, sometimes everything from the vanquished.” (p. 699) <br /><br />As noted in the first paragraph, the perspective in this epic novel jumps around more than a flea on a dog’s back. It is very late in the volume before one finds out the result of the first major event, but even then, the result isn’t totally clear. And, at the end, there is a masterpiece of confusion—a marvelous piece of <i>maskirovka</i>, Soviet deception. Still, <i>In the First Circle</i> is an intensely insightful and satisfying book. It is hardly “western” in its presentation, but it is intriguing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2202368614528152209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_578248848" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/578248848" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18948588-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569598891p2/18948588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/578248848?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/18948588-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=r-r">r-r</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=read-again">read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=three-star-yes">three-star-yes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer578248848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9974309334440757076"><i>"The First Circle"</i> or<i> "In the First Circle"</i> whichever you prefer I suppose, is a novel by Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn first released in 1968. A fuller version of the book was published in English in 2009. The novel tells the story of the occupants of a "sharashka" (a research and development facility made of inmates) located in the Moscow suburbs.<br/><br/>Ok, I'll tell you my thoughts as I was reading this book. I don't know much about communism or socialism, in fact I can never remember which is which and wh</span>
  <span id="freeText9974309334440757076" style="display:none"><i>"The First Circle"</i> or<i> "In the First Circle"</i> whichever you prefer I suppose, is a novel by Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn first released in 1968. A fuller version of the book was published in English in 2009. The novel tells the story of the occupants of a "sharashka" (a research and development facility made of inmates) located in the Moscow suburbs.<br /><br />Ok, I'll tell you my thoughts as I was reading this book. I don't know much about communism or socialism, in fact I can never remember which is which and what the differences are at all, and if I do go look up and read about them you can be sure that before long I will have once again forgotten which is which and what the differences are. To my mind communism sounds kind of nice for awhile. Everyone should be the same - no really wealthy people, no really poor people either. No mansions, but also no slums, just nice houses for all of us. Doctors, lawyers, lumber workers (like my son), house builders (like my husband), we all get the same salary. Well it doesn't work in <i>"The First Circle".</i> In <i>"The First Circle"</i> as far as I can tell almost everyone is extremely poor and extremely miserable and go around with no hope at all. Meanwhile the very few wealthy people left are extremely rich and extremely afraid that at any minute they will be arrested like everyone else is sooner or later, and they also go around with no hope at all. <br /><br />Another thought I had reading this book was, why are people so horrible to each other? Some of the things people did in this book just amazed me, but I finally figured it out. I think the reason one person would do such horrible things to the next person is so that the same horrible things wouldn't be done to him. Not for a while anyway, but it did seem like everyone, no matter who they were, prison guards, engineers, scientists, government employees, whoever you can think of, no matter how important they were at that moment, knew that eventually they would be arrested for something, anything at all. <br /><br />And that's what the book is about for me anyway. Awful things happening to people, mostly in prison or because of the prison. Our prisoners in this book are at a "special prison" named Mavrino named after the nearby village that is now a part of Moscow. In the first chapter a prisoner just arrived from the concentration camps says this of Mavrino:<br /><br /><i>"my head is spinning from this sudden change. I've lived 52 years, I've recovered from fatal illnesses, I've been married to pretty women, I've had sons, I've received academic prizes - but never have I been so blessedly happy as I am today! Where have I landed? They won't be driving me out into icy water tomorrow! An ounce and a half of butter! Black bread - out on the table! They don't forbid books! You can shave yourself! The guards don't beat the zeks. What kind of great day is this? What kind of gleaming summit? Maybe I've died? Maybe this is a dream? Perhaps I'm in heaven."<br />"No, dear sir," said Rubin, "you are, just as you were previously, in hell. But you have risen to its best and highest circle - the first circle..."</i><br /><br />I wonder how you recover from fatal illnesses. The Mavrino sharashka prisoners or zeks, work on technical projects to assist state security agencies and almost all of them know they are much better off than those who have been sent to the gulag prisons or forced labor camps. I will agree that these places, where it seemed like they worked people to death, seemed much worse than the sharashka, but as the character Rubin said <i>"you are, just as you were previously, in hell."</i><br /><br />I was often astonished at the reasons given for someone to be arrested and sentenced for five, ten or even twenty-five years in one prison or another. There were men who had fought for Russia during World War II and had been captured by the Germans, when finally released they returned to Russia only to be arrested because they were believed to be spies. They were asked:<br /><br /><i>"Why did you return to your country if you were not recruited by the enemy?"</i><br /><br />I wonder how I would have answered that. There was a New Year's Eve party where the guests had been arrested, the book tells us:<br /><br /><i>"They shot those who held firm, they shot those who capitulated."</i><br /><br />There is one man, a loyal communist, who is called into Party headquarters to inform on his cousin. He is asked whether his cousin is involved in any anti-party movements or some such thing. Now if he tries to protect his cousin and answers' no' he will be arrested whether his cousin was guilty or not (he was), but being a loyal Party member he tells the truth. He is arrested anyway. Another time they are trying to find the person who called to warn a friend of his possible arrest, they get it narrowed down to possibly two different people, or possibly anyone else in the country, but not being sure which, they just arrest them both. If there is a second book they will probably get around to arresting everyone else in the country. <br /><br />I found the parts about Joseph Stalin fascinating and was left wondering if these two men hated each other. Considering the things Solzhenitsyn says about Stalin, and also considering that Solzhenitsyn spent quite a lot of his life either in prison himself or exiled from Russia altogether, I have a feeling there certainly were no friendly feelings between the two, ever. Here are some of the things Solzhenitsyn says about Stalin:<br /><br /><i>"In prison for two years without investigation or trial, he lived as he had lived previously, solely according to the latest Party directives, and as before, he deified the <b>Wise Teacher</b>."<br /><br />"But management, if the project to be managed was obviously ill-starred, would never put him back in the good graces of the <b>Best Friend of Communications Workers"</b><br /><br />"But the portraits of the <b>Great Generalissimo</b> grew largest and fastest of all."<br /><br />"And in Abakumov's office the <b>Most Brilliant Strategist of All Times and Peoples</b> was portrayed on a canvas fifteen feet tall..."</i><br /><br />It became interesting just to find out how many names Solzhenitsyn could come up with to call Stalin. One more thing he says about Stalin:<br /><br /><i>"This man's name filled the world's newspapers, was uttered out by thousands of announcers in hundreds of languages, cried out by speakers at the beginning and the end of speeches, sung by the tender young voices of Pioneers, and proclaimed by bishops. This man's name was baked on the lips of dying prisoners of war, on the swollen gums of camp prisoners. It had been given to a multitude of cities and squares, streets and boulevards, palaces, universities, schools, sanatoriums, mountain ranges, canals, factories, mines, state and collective farms, battleships, icebreakers, fishing boats, shoemakers' artels, nursery schools - and a group of Moscow journalists had proposed that it be given also to the Volga and to the moon."</i><br /><br />OK, I'm done with Stalin and with prisoners and with the book altogether. I'm giving it three stars because the things going on in it were so awful and I never could judge a book on the writing style and all that, just on how I felt reading it. I do almost wish there was a second one, there are a lot of characters in the novel that I would like to know how their lives end up and right now I don't know. Go ahead and read it just don't expect too many light hearted moments.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9974309334440757076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_263786174" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263786174" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4930019-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575967706p2/4930019.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/263786174?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/4930019-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4930019-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4930019-sara?shelf=russia">russia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer263786174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1377876862029500970">For those of you considering reading this – apparently there is a 'real version' so let me explain. I read (after already getting through most of this edition) that Solzhenitsyn first wrote this novel with 9 more chapters and a different beginning (Volodin calls the US embassy to warn of the USSR's attempt at nuclear capabilities) but he edited it down because he thought a lightened version might be more publishable in the USSR. It was not (shocking, I know ;-). So The First Circle wasn't publis</span>
  <span id="freeText1377876862029500970" style="display:none">For those of you considering reading this – apparently there is a 'real version' so let me explain. I read (after already getting through most of this edition) that Solzhenitsyn first wrote this novel with 9 more chapters and a different beginning (Volodin calls the US embassy to warn of the USSR's attempt at nuclear capabilities) but he edited it down because he thought a lightened version might be more publishable in the USSR. It was not (shocking, I know ;-). So The First Circle wasn't published in its original form until 1978 in Russia and not until 2009 in the US. I wish I had known this before launching into this tome because I would have much rather read it as it was intended by Solzhenitsyn. <br /><br />(btw- the 96 chapter version in English is available through Harper Perennial)<br /><br />That aside, I really enjoyed this novel. It reminded me of many different things, ranging from Solzhenitsyn's other works to the comedy show The Office (roll your eyes if you must but you'll see what I mean when you read it). The sharashka zeks are well educated and are working (alongside non prisoners) on top-secret Soviet projects, the one around which this story revolves being a voice identifier. Initially, I found myself a little lost with the technical back and forth between Solzhenitsyn's cast of engineers and mathematicians, but I soon zoned it out and focused on the meat of this story – how to maintain your humanity when it is stripped away. <br /><br />Though these zeks inhabit the 'first circle' of the Soviet prison system, ('limbo' in Dante's work) the effects of losing your freedom can be just as severe as those in more labor-intensive/deadly camps. Some characters even openly admit that they would prefer physical labor to being forced to work on projects that they don't morally agree with, so the main struggle throughout this novel is whether to cooperate or not and at what cost. (yay for Nerzhin!!!) The side to which you find yourself leaning at the end (meaning – do you find it a happy ending or a sad ending) should tell you a lot about your own moral standards. <br /><br />The entire novel, though over 500 pages takes place in just a few days and so your insight into these characters is deep and you will find yourself self reflecting the entire time, which can be a good thing or a bad thing - I couldn't read this at night because it got my mind going too much to fall asleep.<br /><br />Oh – and the portrait of Stalin is a total winner ;-)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1377876862029500970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review263786174">

<div id="review_1711736636" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1711736636" />
    <a title="Paul Gaya Ochieng Simeon Juma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma"><img alt="Paul Gaya Ochieng Simeon Juma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453048287p2/19439030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1711736636?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Gaya Ochieng Simeon Juma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Gaya Ochieng Simeon Juma" href="/user/show/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma">Paul Gaya Ochieng Simeon Juma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/13055371-the-first-circle">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=cultural">cultural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=nobel-winner">nobel-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=very-long">very-long</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=books-i-will-forever-remeber">books-i-will-forever-remeber</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19439030-paul-gaya-ochieng-simeon-juma?shelf=prison-life">prison-life</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1711736636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer289932606631046877">'The First Circle' is a novel to remember. I now understand why the writer is compared to Fyodor Dostoyevsky. A nobel winner himself, the writer has proved that he deserves the title.<br/><br/>The novel takes us into the Sharaska, a Russian prison that houses the most 'dangerous' prisoners in the country. Also known as political prisoners. Among them are Nerzhin, Solodgin, Rubin, Adamson, Bobynin ete. The offenders are referred to as 'zeks'. Most of them are educated and have attained the highest level of</span>
  <span id="freeText289932606631046877" style="display:none">'The First Circle' is a novel to remember. I now understand why the writer is compared to Fyodor Dostoyevsky. A nobel winner himself, the writer has proved that he deserves the title.<br /><br />The novel takes us into the Sharaska, a Russian prison that houses the most 'dangerous' prisoners in the country. Also known as political prisoners. Among them are Nerzhin, Solodgin, Rubin, Adamson, Bobynin ete. The offenders are referred to as 'zeks'. Most of them are educated and have attained the highest level of penmanship in their profession. They are imprisoned because they don't agree with Stalin's policies and views. <br /><br />Stalin is portrayed as a paranoid dictator who has no friends besides Hitler. He arrests and imprisons both the guilty and innocent citizens indiscriminately. Prison, as portrayed is a hard place to all of them. They have to work long hours without pay, live together in a filthy place etc. The tyrant is feared by prisoners and guards alike. <br /><br />Trials at the time are not fair. Prisoners are tried and convicted on hearsay evidence alone. Moreover, they may be tried in absentia without being required to give their side of the story. Afterwards they are separated from their families and their work. They are not allowed to read or write any material while in the prison. Foreign books are also forbidden. All that remains are memories of their past and hope or despair for the uncertain future.<br /><br />Strength is the only virtue that carries them on. Despite their circumstances, they can afford to laugh and be happy in the company of each other, I would recommend the book to everyone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="289932606631046877" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1323157-keith?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1323157-keith?shelf=a-banned">a-banned</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207453958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13552864557301425213">I'd been meaning to dig into Solzhenitsyn since I was a teenager and first became captivated by the haunting song "Mother Russia" by the folk-prog band Renaissance. Recent events in US politics have put a finer point on that resolve, and made him seem more relevant than at any time since the Glasnost reforms.<br/><br/>As such, this was a depressingly fascinating read. Perhaps not quite so depressing as <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/261745.One_Day_in_the_Life_of_Ivan_Denisovich' title='One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich by Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn' rel='nofollow noopener'>One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</a>
</i>, which covers one winter day (naturally) in a deadly Siberian</span>
  <span id="freeText13552864557301425213" style="display:none">I'd been meaning to dig into Solzhenitsyn since I was a teenager and first became captivated by the haunting song "Mother Russia" by the folk-prog band Renaissance. Recent events in US politics have put a finer point on that resolve, and made him seem more relevant than at any time since the Glasnost reforms.<br /><br />As such, this was a depressingly fascinating read. Perhaps not quite so depressing as <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/261745.One_Day_in_the_Life_of_Ivan_Denisovich" title="One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich by Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn" rel="nofollow noopener">One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</a>
</i>, which covers one winter day (naturally) in a deadly Siberian labor camp rather than the three December days in a comparatively cushy "Sharashka," or scientific prison complex, where at least they understood that these mostly-political prisoners need to be well fed and not frozen if they are to accomplish the engineering tasks set them. Still, it's prison, and with the perverse Stalinist Soviet interpretation of the Kafkaesque that maddens one's life, and the continual possibility of being sent back to the labor camps, or perhaps even executed outright, forever hanging over one's head.<br /><br />For all that, when the junta finally rounds me up, I'd still rather this brand of torture than the other, and I feel now like I'll be better prepared for it than most. Completely worth the heartache, even if America never slides back quite that far under Putin's rule.</span>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MountainShelby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MountainShelby" href="/user/show/4112635-mountainshelby">MountainShelby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060916831" href="/book/show/977094.The_First_Circle">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4112635-mountainshelby?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4112635-mountainshelby?shelf=russian">russian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer250912825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14260965088095162626">First, do find the 2009 edition if you want to read all 96 chapters. The 1991 version I read, while a good translation, only had 87 or so chapters--apparently the former USSR abridged earlier editions. (The story turned on the release of nuclear or atomic secrets, which I believe was revised for public release as medical secrets.)<br/><br/>If you are thinking of reading this book, do so. This book is a monumental achievement, and it is well worth the effort of reading/studying. Having said that, this book</span>
  <span id="freeText14260965088095162626" style="display:none">First, do find the 2009 edition if you want to read all 96 chapters. The 1991 version I read, while a good translation, only had 87 or so chapters--apparently the former USSR abridged earlier editions. (The story turned on the release of nuclear or atomic secrets, which I believe was revised for public release as medical secrets.)<br /><br />If you are thinking of reading this book, do so. This book is a monumental achievement, and it is well worth the effort of reading/studying. Having said that, this book was a challenge for me, as I am not a fan of ensemble books (many characters) and a rather thin plotline. I enjoy reading about 1 to 3 characters, and while I do not need or even enjoy action adventure thrillers, I need some kind of forward movement. Set over the course of three days, the book is more an exploration of life for the educated GULAG technical prisoners (of a white collar work camp, so to speak), and it is not like anything I have ever read. The final chapter is exceptionally moving in a quiet, noble way--I would rate the ending as in the top three saddest of all endings I have ever read. I was so profoundly impacted by these characters and their hopeless plight . . . <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14260965088095162626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152126078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12841763700987873380">This is a truly amazing book. Solzhenitsyn tells the stories of a variety of people in Stalinist Russia: several zeks, people who work in the prison, a zek's wife, even Stalin has a few chapters. Each story focuses on the development of the person and discusses their innermost thoughts and beliefs as well as their internal struggles. This could be seen as a time capsule of this time period. Solzhenitsyn records social mores, philosophical questions, literary debates, bureaucratic limitations, pr</span>
  <span id="freeText12841763700987873380" style="display:none">This is a truly amazing book. Solzhenitsyn tells the stories of a variety of people in Stalinist Russia: several zeks, people who work in the prison, a zek's wife, even Stalin has a few chapters. Each story focuses on the development of the person and discusses their innermost thoughts and beliefs as well as their internal struggles. This could be seen as a time capsule of this time period. Solzhenitsyn records social mores, philosophical questions, literary debates, bureaucratic limitations, prison organization, legal considerations, etc. This book has it all. <br /><br />This was my first book by Solzhenitsyn. I had been aware of him for a few years before I was actually able to read any of his writing. I first saw his picture in my local town paper while I was a teenager. He was at a funeral and it talked about him being a famous writer and even drew attention to his deeply lined face. I was simply entranced by the image of this "dissident writer" and looked for his books for a few years before I managed to get a copy of The First Circle. It re-established the special love I have always felt towards all things Russian. <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12841763700987873380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1022676725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3238186599187383684">This was the first book by Alexander Solzhenitsyn I read. I was first sceptical about the book expecting not more than a lot of nagging from a persecuted dissident. However, the book turned out to be quite fascinating due to its captivating plot and vivid descriptions. Solzhenitsyn portrayed a variety of characters who had different backgrounds and political views. I was excited to note that the topics of their political discourse 50 years ago (and indeed 100 years ago, as also described in the </span>
  <span id="freeText3238186599187383684" style="display:none">This was the first book by Alexander Solzhenitsyn I read. I was first sceptical about the book expecting not more than a lot of nagging from a persecuted dissident. However, the book turned out to be quite fascinating due to its captivating plot and vivid descriptions. Solzhenitsyn portrayed a variety of characters who had different backgrounds and political views. I was excited to note that the topics of their political discourse 50 years ago (and indeed 100 years ago, as also described in the book) were very similar to the ones that are topical in today’s Russia. What was the most interesting to me is the description of engineers working in prison conditions, where they were meant to develop the technologies to serve props to the regime which was hated by many of them. In the book, their fates are contrasted to the lives of allegedly free people, who used the benefits traded by the loyalty to the regime but who were still constantly afraid to lose their position due to arbitraristic government. What option would you choose?</span>
  <a data-text-id="3238186599187383684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UeRBG4pMo7qBNDem2Q0Lp5/y/8LJnkzBQwYR4tN3ufIc061iKk97YkO9QRWHCEFhTV3DeZg+VkA7f11Zhv1WiA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FgDPqIyea2uJ26lVmkqBLbaUg2gRQcwWvL/EedlRomNbNyPRLJ2zs0tS3+bET8vrZDu/00Dh1pfExojCjNtNGQ==')})
  }

A portrait of the conflict between the powerful and the powerless, of one man’s fierce reaction to injustice, and of one woman’s stoical strength, the novel captures the horrors of the Great Depression and probes into the very nature of equality and justice in America. The 1st three volumes were written from 1925 to \'32 &amp; published in the Soviet magazine October in 1928–32. The 4th volume was finished in 1940. The English translation of the 1st three volumes appeared under this title in 1934. The novel is considered one of the most significant works of Russian literature in the 20th century. It depicts the lives &amp; struggles of Don Cossacks during WWI, the Russian Revolution &amp; Russian Civil War. In 1965, Sholokhov was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature for this novel.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/19771050.Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn">About Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14253693337784247786"><b>Aleksandr Isayevich Solzhenitsyn</b> (Russian: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13622681._____________________________' title='Александр Иса́евич Солженицын' rel='nofollow noopener'>Александр Иса́евич Солженицын</a>) was a Soviet and Russian novelist, dramatist, and historian. Through his writings he helped to make the world aware of the Gulag, the Soviet Union's forced labor camp system—particularly <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i> and <i>One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</i>, two of his best-known works. <br/><br/>Solzhenitsyn was awarded the Nobel Prize in </span>
  <span id="freeText14253693337784247786" style="display:none"><b>Aleksandr Isayevich Solzhenitsyn</b> (Russian: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13622681._____________________________" title="Александр Иса́евич Солженицын" rel="nofollow noopener">Александр Иса́евич Солженицын</a>) was a Soviet and Russian novelist, dramatist, and historian. Through his writings he helped to make the world aware of the Gulag, the Soviet Union's forced labor camp system—particularly <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i> and <i>One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</i>, two of his best-known works. <br /><br />Solzhenitsyn was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1970. He was exiled from the Soviet Union in 1974 and returned to Russia in 1994. Solzhenitsyn was the father of Ignat Solzhenitsyn, a conductor and pianist.<br /><br />AKA<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16981844.Alexander_Solzenitsyn" title="Alexander Solzenitsyn" rel="nofollow noopener">Alexander Solzenitsyn</a> (English, alternate)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17891441.____________________" title="Αλεξάντρ Σολζενίτσιν" rel="nofollow noopener">Αλεξάντρ Σολζενίτσιν</a> (Greek)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14253693337784247786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
